Black has a pawn on e5 which is currently unsupported by other pawns (d7 and f7 support e6, not e5), but it is safe for now.

Pushing the e-pawn to e4 (`e5e4`) is a strong strategic move. It gains space in the center, restricts White's development (particularly the knight on g1 which cannot easily go to f3), and forces White to react. White's d2 and e2 pawns are still on their starting squares, so the e4 pawn is not immediately under attack. This move seizes the initiative and capitalizes on White's early deviation with h4.

The Black pawn on e2 can capture the White Bishop on f1.
Since f1 is on the 1st rank, the pawn will promote upon capture.
Promoting to a Queen is the standard and strongest choice.
Although the White King can capture the new Queen on f1 (Kxf1), this results in an exchange of a Black pawn for a White Bishop.
Since a Bishop is worth more than a pawn (3 points vs 1 point), this is a favorable trade for Black.
Additionally, forcing the White King to move to f1 exposes it and removes a defensive piece.
The move is legal and strategically sound.

However, the most compelling move is to relocate this Knight to a more active square. The square **e4** is empty and safe from White's pieces (the White King on e2 cannot reach it, and the White Bishop on c5 cannot attack it).

By playing **f2e4**:
1.  The Knight escapes the Bishop's attack.
2.  The Knight lands on a strong central square.
3.  The Knight attacks the White Rook on c3.
4.  The White Rook on c3 appears to be undefended (the White King is on e2, too far to defend; the White Bishop on c5 moves diagonally and cannot defend the Rook on the same file).
5.  Therefore, this move threatens to win the White Rook (`Nxc3`), gaining significant material advantage.

Other moves like `f2g4` (capturing the pawn) would result in the Knight being captured by the pawn on h5 (`hxg4`), losing material. `f2d3` would allow the White King to capture the Knight.

Thus, `f2e4` is the best move.

If the White King moves to d6, I can follow up with `Qe5+` forcing the King to d7, and then `Qd6#`. If the King moves to d4, I can continue the attack with `Qe5+`. Although c4 remains an escape square, `Rac8` significantly restricts the King and sets up a mating net. Other moves like `Qxg4` or `Qxh5` are met by pawn captures, losing the Queen. `Re5` is met by `Kxe5`. Therefore, `a8c8` is the best move.

Player_Black (to Proxy):



The board analysis shows that Black is under pressure. The White King is active on c5, and White has pawns on b6 and c6. The Black pawn on b7 is attacked by the White pawn on c6. If Black does not address this threat, White will likely play `cxb7`, winning a pawn and creating a dangerous passed pawn structure.

The legal moves provided are `h8g8`, `h8h7`, `h8g7`, `b7c6`, and `g5g4`.
- Moving the King (`h8g8`, `h8h7`, `h8g7`) does not address the threat to the b7 pawn. White would simply capture `cxb7`.
- Playing `g5g4` advances the g-pawn but ignores the threat. White would play `cxb7`, leaving Black down a pawn with a winning position for White.
- Playing `b7c6` captures the attacking White pawn on c6. This saves the b7 pawn (by removing the threat) and gains material (capturing a pawn). Although White can likely recapture with `Kxc6`, this move maintains material equality and is the most resilient option compared to losing a pawn for free.

Therefore, the best move is to capture the pawn on c6.

make_move b7c6

The move `c6c5` advances the c-pawn. This pawn is on the c-file, directly in front of the White King on c3. By playing `c6c5`, the Black pawn attacks the squares b4 and d4, which are adjacent to the White King. This restricts the White King's movement and creates immediate tension. Although the White Pawn on b6 is a major threat, `c6c5` is the most active move that creates counterplay and complicates the position. The move `g5g4` advances a pawn on the other side of the board, which is less effective against the immediate threat on the queenside. King moves are passive.

Therefore, `c6c5` is the best move.

make_move c6c5

Player_Black (to Proxy):



The current board state shows a King and Pawn endgame.
- **White**: King on a1, Pawn on b6, Pawn on h5.
- **Black**: King on h7, Pawn on h6, Pawn on g5, Pawn on c4.

**Analysis:**
1.  **Kingside**: The Black King is active on h7. Black has pawns on g5 and h6. The White Pawn on h5 is blocked by the Black Pawn on h6. The Black Pawn on g5 is a passed pawn and is far from the White King. Pushing this pawn (`g5g4`) creates a strong threat of promotion. The White King is on a1, which is too far away to stop the g-pawn in time.
2.  **Queenside**: Black has a pawn on c4. Pushing it (`c4c3`) creates a passed pawn there as well. However, the White King is closer to the queenside. If Black pushes the c-pawn, the White King might be able to intercept or capture it (e.g., via b1 or b2).
3.  **Strategy**: The most decisive move is to advance the g-pawn. The White King is on the opposite side of the board and cannot stop it. The White Pawn on h5 cannot influence the g-file effectively.

**Move Selection:**
- `g5g4`: Advances the g-pawn. It is a passed pawn race, and Black is winning easily.
- `c4c3`: Also advances a passed pawn, but the White King is closer and might stop it.
- King moves (`h7h8`, `h7g8`, `h7g7`): Passive. The King is already safe and active enough. Pawn advances are more urgent to secure the win.

Therefore, `g5g4` is the best move.

make_move g5g4

The current board state shows Black has a pawn at c2, one square away from promotion. The White King is at b2, adjacent to the promotion square c1.
- Promoting to a Queen (`c2c1q`) places the Queen at c1. Since the Queen attacks diagonals, it attacks the square b2 where the White King is located. This puts the White King in check. The King cannot capture the Queen because it would be moving into check. This is a winning move as Black gains a Queen and checks the opponent.
- Promoting to a Rook (`c2c1r`) or Knight (`c2c1n`) would place the piece on c1, but neither attacks b2. The White King could capture the new piece.
- Promoting to a Bishop (`c2c1b`) would also check the King (as c1 and b2 are on the same diagonal), but a Queen is more powerful.

Therefore, promoting to a Queen is the best move.

make_move c2c1q

The board shows a winning endgame for Black. Black has a Queen and a pawn, while White has a King and a pawn. The White King is on e3, and the White pawn is on h5. The Black Queen is on f6, and the Black pawn is on h6.

The move **f6g5** (Queen from f6 to g5) is a strong move for several reasons:
1.  **Check**: It delivers a check to the White King on e3 (diagonal g5-f4-e3).
2.  **Attack**: It places the Queen on a square attacking the White pawn on h5.
3.  **Forcing**: Since it is a check, White must respond to the check, limiting their options.
4.  **Winning Material**: After the White King moves, Black can capture the pawn on h5 (Qxh5), further increasing the material advantage and simplifying the position to a trivial Queen vs King endgame.

Other checks like Qf3+ or Qf4+ would allow the White King to capture the Queen, which would be a blunder. Qe6+ is a check but does not win the pawn immediately and allows the King more freedom. Therefore, f6g5 is the best move.

make_move f6g5

It centralizes the Knight, controls important squares (e3, c3, f4), and clears the f6 square, allowing the Queen to capture the pawn on g5 on the next move (threatening ...Qxg5). White has no good way to defend g5 or prevent the threat without making concessions (like moving the pawn to g4, which might weaken the position).

Other Knight moves like ...Nh5 or ...Ne4 are possible but less optimal (...Ne4 allows f3xe4 losing the Knight, ...Nh5 is less central). Pawn moves or Bishop moves are slower and do not exploit the weakness on g5 immediately.

Therefore, the best move is **f6d5**.

The board analysis shows that Black has a pawn on h3 (Rank 3) which is under attack by the White Bishop on f1. If Black does not move this pawn, White can capture it with `Bxh3`. The only legal move for this pawn is to advance to h2 (`h3h2`).

Moving the pawn to h2 (`h3h2`) achieves several objectives:
1.  **Saves the pawn**: The pawn moves out of the Bishop's attack (since the Bishop is on a light square and h2 is a dark square).
2.  **Attacks the Rook**: The pawn on h2 attacks the White Rook on g1 diagonally. The Rook must move or be captured.
3.  **Promotion Threat**: The pawn is one step away from promotion on h1.

Even if White defends by moving the Rook to h1 to block the promotion, Black maintains a strong passed pawn on h2 and has gained tempo by attacking the Rook. Other moves like `g6g5` or `a6a5` are less forcing and risk losing the h3 pawn.

Therefore, `h3h2` is the best move.

make_move h3h2

Player_Black (to Proxy):



The position shows Black to move. Black has a material advantage (two rooks vs one rook, and more pawns), but the White King is somewhat exposed on c2. The White Bishop on c3 is active, eyeing the long diagonal and controlling key squares like f6.

Let's analyze the candidate moves:
1.  **Pawn moves:**
    *   `a6a5`: This moves the pawn to a5. However, the White Bishop on c3 can capture on a5 (`Bxa5`) because the path c3-b4-a5 is clear and a5 is undefended. This would lose a pawn for Black.
    *   `h6h5`: This moves the pawn to h5. While defended by the g6 pawn, this move opens the h-file for the White Rook on h2. White could play `Rh6` followed by `Rh8+`, forcing the Black King to move and potentially creating complications.
    *   `g6g5`: This moves the pawn to g5. The square g5 is not attacked by any White piece (the Bishop on c3 attacks f6, g7, h8, but not g5). The pawn on g5 is defended by the pawn on h6. This move gains space on the kingside, restricts White's options slightly, and does not expose Black to tactics. It is a safe and active move.

2.  **Rook moves:**
    *   `Rd2+`: The Rook on d8 could move to d2 to check the King. However, the King can capture (`Kxd2`) or the Knight on b1 can capture (`Nxd2`). This would lose a Rook.
    *   `Rd3`, `Rd4`, `Rd5`: These squares are controlled by White pieces (King, Bishop, or pawns), so moving a Rook there would likely result in losing it.
    *   `Rf2`: The square f2 is defended by the White Rook on h2, so `Rxf2` would follow.
    *   `Rc8`: Moving the Rook to c8 is blocked by the Black pawn on c5 (if moving from f8) or just passive (if moving from d8). Also, the c-file is blocked by the pawn on c5.

3.  **King moves:**
    *   `Kh7` or `Kf7`: These moves are safe but passive. The King is relatively safe on g8 behind the pawn chain. Moving it might expose it unnecessarily.

**Conclusion:**
The move `g6g5` is the best option. It improves Black's pawn structure, gains space, and is safe from immediate tactics. It avoids the pitfalls of `a6a5` (losing a pawn) and `h6h5` (opening the h-file).

make_move g6g5

White has a King on d3 and Pawns on b3, c4.
The goal is to promote a pawn.
The g-pawn is on g5 (Rank 5). It is a passed pawn (no white pawns on f, g, h files).
The a-pawn is on a6 (Rank 6). It is also a candidate, but the b3 pawn controls a4, potentially blocking it.
The g-pawn is closer to promotion (4 steps vs 5 steps for the a-pawn).
Pushing the g-pawn creates a strong passed pawn that the White King is too far away to stop easily.
The move `g5g4` advances the g-pawn to the 4th rank.

h8g8,h8h7,h8g7,h6h5,a6a5,g4g3

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
Player_Black (to Proxy):



The position shows Black to move. Black has pawns on a7, a6, c6, g4, h6 and the King on h8. White has pawns on b3, c4 and the King on e2.
The g-pawn is advanced but vulnerable to the White King (e2-f3-g4).
The a-pawn is on a6, with a pawn on a7 behind it.
Pushing the a-pawn (`a6a5`) creates an outside passed pawn.
This is a classic endgame strategy. The White King is on the kingside (e2). By creating a passed pawn on the queenside (a-file), Black forces the White King to choose between dealing with the g-pawn and stopping the a-pawn.
Given the distances, it is difficult for the White King to stop both.
If the White King goes after the g-pawn, he will be far from the a-file, allowing the a-pawn to promote.
If the White King goes after the a-pawn, the g-pawn might promote or cause trouble.
Even if White King stops the a-pawn, the activity created is significant.
Also, `a6a5` is a safe move, not losing material immediately.
`g4g3` loses the pawn to `Kf3`.
`h8g7` is a good move but `a6a5` creates an immediate threat and utilizes the outside passed pawn principle.

Therefore, `a6a5` is the best move.

make_move a6a5

The White King could potentially move to a2 (if not blocked) or capture the pawn if it advances, or create a stalemate situation if the King is trapped in the corner. Specifically, if Black plays `a4a3` and White plays `Ka1`, pushing the pawn further might lead to a stalemate if the King has no legal moves. Additionally, `Ka2` would attack the pawn at a3.
2.  **Analyze the h-pawn (h4):** The h-pawn is also a passed pawn and is far away from the White King at b1. The White King cannot reach the h-file in time to stop the pawn from promoting. Pushing `h4h3` is safe and leads directly to promotion (`h3h2`, then `h2h1=Q`), securing a win.
3.  **Other moves:** King moves (`e8f8`, etc.) are passive and do not create immediate threats. The Knight at b8 is far from the action.

3.  **Comparison**: The a-pawn is on a4. Pushing it (`a4a3`) is less effective because the White King on c1 is close to the a-file and can intercept it easily.

Therefore, pushing the h-pawn is the strongest move, creating a decisive promotion threat while keeping the King safe on e8.

Player_Black (to Proxy):



The position shows that White has played an early and somewhat dubious **g4** (Grob Attack), neglecting the center and weakening the kingside. Black has responded with **...e5** and **...Nc6**, establishing a presence in the center.

As Black, the priority is to develop pieces, control the center, and exploit White's weaknesses.
1.  **Analyze the g4 pawn:** The white pawn on g4 is currently unsupported. The white Knight on h3 does not defend g4 (it defends f4 and g5). The Queen's diagonal is blocked by the pawn on e2. The pawns on f2 and h2 defend g3, not g4.
2.  **Evaluate candidate moves:**
    *   **Nf6 (g8f6):** This is the most principled developing move. It brings the knight to a strong square, controls the center (e4, d5), and importantly, **attacks the undefended pawn on g4**. If White fails to defend or move the pawn, Black wins material. Even if White pushes **g5**, the knight can retreat to a strong square like **e4** or **d5**.
    *   **d6 (d7d6):** A solid move supporting e5, but less active than Nf6.
    *   **Bc5 (f8c5):** Aggressive, but Nf6 is more flexible and safe.
    *   **Pawn attacks (h5 or f5):** These would be premature and likely lose a pawn as the squares are unsupported.

**Conclusion:** The move **Nf6** is best. It develops a piece, fights for the center, and immediately creates a threat against the loose g4 pawn.

Promoting on c1 (`c1=Q`) would place the new Queen on c1, where it would be immediately captured by the White Rook on b1 (`Rxc1`), resulting in a loss of material (trading a pawn for nothing, or effectively losing the pawn).
However, the pawn can capture the Rook on b1 (`c2xb1`).
This move captures a major piece (Rook) and promotes the pawn to a Queen.
The move is `c2b1q` (Pawn on c2 captures on b1 and promotes to Queen).
This wins a full Rook and gives Black an extra Queen, leading to a winning material advantage.

Analysis of the move **b1e4**:
1.  **Move Validity**: The Black Queen on b1 can move diagonally to e4 (b1-c2-d3-e4). The squares c2 and d3 are empty, so the path is clear. The destination e4 is empty.
2.  **Check**: Upon moving to e4, the Queen attacks the White King on e2 vertically (e4-e3-e2). The square e3 is empty, so the check is valid.
3.  **Safety**: The Queen on e4 is safe. The White King is too far to capture it. No White pawns (on f2, d4, c4, b3, a4, h2) can capture on e4. There are no other White pieces.
4.  **Forcing Nature**: This is a check, forcing the White King to move.
    *   The King cannot move to e3, f3, d3, or e1 because these squares are attacked by the Queen on e4.
    *   The King cannot stay on e2 (check).
    *   The King cannot move to f2 (occupied by own pawn).
    *   The King's possible moves are restricted to **d1**, **d2**, or **f1**.
5.  **Mating Net**:
    *   If White plays **Kd1**, Black plays **Qe1#** (mate). The Queen on e1 is protected by the Knight on g2, preventing capture. All escape squares (c1, c2, d2, e2) are covered by the Queen.
    *   If White plays **Kd2**, Black plays **Qe2#** (mate). The Queen on e2 is protected by the Knight on g2. All escape squares are covered.
    *   If White plays **Kf1**, Black plays **Qe1#** (mate). The Queen on e1 is protected by the Knight on g2. All escape squares (g1, e2, d1) are covered or blocked.

This move forces a checkmate in two moves. It is the best move.

The board analysis shows that Black has a very strong position with a Bishop on g1 (deep in White's territory) and active pieces in the center. The White King is on e1, somewhat exposed.

The best move is **e5g4**.
1.  **Capture Material:** The Black Knight on e5 captures the White Pawn on g4.
2.  **Create a Threat:** The Knight lands on g4, creating a deadly threat of **Nxf2#** (Checkmate).
    *   If Black plays `Nxf2+`, the White King on e1 is in check.
    *   The King cannot move to e2 (blocked by own pawn) or f1 (blocked by own bishop).
    *   The King cannot capture the Knight on f2 because it is protected by the Black Bishop on g1 (g1 and f2 are on the same diagonal).
    *   The King's escape square d1 is attacked by the Knight on f2.
    *   The only escape might be d2, but the King would be extremely exposed and Black would maintain a winning advantage with the Knight on f2 and Bishop on g1 coordinating.
3.  **Safety:** The Knight on g4 is safe from capture by White pieces.

Therefore, capturing the pawn and threatening mate is the winning move.

make_move e5g4

Player_Black (to Proxy):



The current board state shows that it is Black's turn. The Black King is on e8 and is in check by the White Queen on e5. The legal moves provided are `e8f8` and `e8d8`.

Analysis of the position:
1.  **Immediate Threat**: The Black King is in check from the White Queen on e5. The King must move to f8 or d8.
2.  **Hanging Rook**: The White Queen on e5 attacks the Black Rook on h8 along the long diagonal (e5-h8). The Rook on h8 is undefended. White will likely capture the Rook on the next move (`Qxh8`).
3.  **D-file Danger**: The Black Pawn on d4 is undefended and attacked by the White Queen and the White Pawn on c5. It is likely to be captured soon. If the d4 pawn falls, the d-file will open up for the White Rook on d1.
4.  **King Safety**:
    *   If the King moves to **d8** (`e8d8`), it places the King on the d-file. Since the d-file is about to open due to the weakness of the d4 pawn, this would put the King in a very dangerous position, potentially facing a direct attack from the White Rook.
    *   If the King moves to **f8** (`e8f8`), it places the King on the f-file. While the Rook on h8 is likely lost, the King on f8 is not on the d-file. If the d-file opens, the King remains safe from the Rook's direct line of fire. Additionally, if White plays `Qxh8+`, the King can move to e7, which is not attacked by the Queen on h8 or other White pieces.

Therefore, moving the King to f8 is the safer option as it avoids the dangerous d-file and maintains better king safety despite the loss of the Rook.

The board shows that it is Black's turn. Black has a Knight on f2 (тЩЮ) which is deeply infiltrated into White's position. The White Rook is on d1 (тЩЦ) and the White King is on e1 (тЩФ). The Knight on f2 attacks the Rook on d1.

If Black plays **Nxd1** (Knight takes d1), the Knight captures the Rook. The White King on e1 is adjacent to d1 and will likely recapture the Knight (Kxd1). This results in an exchange where Black loses a Knight (3 points) and White loses a Rook (5 points), giving Black a material advantage of +2 points.

Other options for the Knight:
- **Nxh3**: Captures the Bishop on h3. However, the Bishop is protected by the Queen on f5 (diagonal f5-h3 is clear via g4). So White would recapture with Qxh3. This is a trade of Knight for Bishop (3 for 3), resulting in no material gain.
- **Ne4**: Moves to an empty square, but the Queen on f5 attacks e4, so White would capture the Knight with Qxe4, losing a piece for nothing.
- **Nxd3**: Captures the pawn on d3, but it is protected by the Queen, so White would recapture with Qxd3, losing a Knight for a Pawn.

Therefore, capturing the Rook on d1 is the best move materially and strategically, as it forces a favorable trade. The move is legal as the Knight is not pinned (checked lines from White pieces to Black King on e8 are clear or blocked).

Player_Black (to Proxy):



The position shows Black to move. Black is down a Queen (White has a Queen on d5, Black does not), so the goal is to survive and look for counterplay or simplification.
The Black King is on g7. The Black Bishop is on g1.
There is a potential weakness on f5 (Black Pawn), which is attacked by the White Bishop on h3 (diagonal h3-g4-f5). If the f5 pawn falls, the King's shelter is compromised.
Also, the White Queen on d5 is active.
Moving the King to f6 (`g7f6`) has several benefits:
1.  It centralizes the King, which is good for endgames.
2.  It defends the f5 pawn (King on f6 protects f5). This prevents White from capturing the pawn with the Bishop (`Bxf5`), which would expose the King.
3.  It moves the King out of the g-file, preventing potential checks or attacks along that file.
4.  It is a safe move; the square f6 is not attacked by White pieces. The White Queen cannot check on f6 safely (any check like `Qe6` or `Qf7` would allow the King to capture the Queen, as those squares are not protected).

Other moves like `g1e3` activate the Bishop but leave the f5 pawn hanging, allowing `Bxf5`, which would be a significant loss of material and safety.
Pawn moves like `b6a5` or `b6b5` lose pawns to the Queen.
`f5f4` opens lines for the White Bishop.

Therefore, `g7f6` is the most solid and principled move.

make_move g7f6

The best square for the Knight is **e7**.

2.  **Effect**: This move opens the d-file, delivering a check from the Queen on d8 to the King on d1.
3.  **Threat**: The Knight on e7 attacks the White Bishop on f5. The Bishop on f5 appears to be undefended.
4.  **Result**: White must respond to the check. White cannot capture the Knight on f3 (exf3) because it is not legal to leave the King in check. After White responds (e.g., moving the King or blocking with a pawn), Black can capture the Bishop on f5 with the Knight (Nxf5), winning a full piece while keeping the Knight on f3 safe.

This move secures the initiative, saves the active Knight on f3, and wins material.

Black's move **c7c6** is a strong positional move.
1.  **Solidifies the center**: It supports the d5 pawn indirectly by controlling key squares and reinforcing the pawn chain.
2.  **Unblocks the Queen**: The pawn on c7 was blocking the long diagonal for the Queen on d8 (d8-a5). Moving it to c6 opens this diagonal, allowing the Queen to potentially enter the game via b6 or a5.
3.  **Prepares expansion**: It prepares for queenside expansion with ...b5 if needed.
4.  **Safety**: It is a safe move that does not expose Black to immediate tactical threats.

Other moves like ...h6 or ...g6 are also possible but less active. Moves like ...Nxd3 or ...Nxf2 are tactical blunders as the Knights would be recaptured for a mere pawn.

Therefore, **c7c6** is the best move.

Black can then proceed to checkmate or promote pawns.

Analysis of the position:
- **Black King** is at **g1**.
- **Black Rook** is at **e1**.
- **White Queen** is at **a1**.
- **White King** is at **f6**.
- **White Pawns** are at **d3** and **g6**.
- **Black Pawns** are at **a2**, **c4**, **f3**, **g3**, **h2**.
- **Black Knight** is at **d6**.
- **Black Queen** is at **e8**.

The Black Rook at e1 is currently pinned by the White Queen at a1 along the first rank (a1-e1-g1). If the Rook moves, the King at g1 would be in check from the Queen. Therefore, the Rook cannot move.
The Black King is not in check, but it is restricted. It cannot move to f1 or h1 because those squares are attacked by the White Queen on the first rank.
The King can move to **f2** or **g2**.
- Moving to **g2** (`g1g2`) leaves the Rook at e1 unprotected and still pinned. White would likely capture the Rook with `Qxe1`, winning material.
- Moving to **f2** (`g1f2`) places the King on f2. The King at f2 protects the square e1. This breaks the pin (or rather, protects the pinned piece), allowing the Rook to move safely in the future. Additionally, f2 is not attacked by any White pieces (Queen at a1, King at f6, Pawns at d3/g6).

Therefore, `g1f2` is the best move to save the Rook and improve the King's safety.
